Predicting Speeding Tickets in Chattanooga, TN with Ludwig

Author: Pete Way


In this example we demonstrate a different process than our previous post, utilizing the same data as presented before for the automated creation of a prediction model with Ludwig.

Within this post, we will:

  • Install Ludwig within a Virtual Environment
  • Create a model defintion yaml file
  • Train a citation prediction model
  • Test the created prediction model
  • Review Accuracy of Training Results
  • Discuss Issues and Drawbacks of Ludwig
  • Summary

Introduction to Ludwig

Ludwig was a creation of Piero Molino during his work with Uber, where several Natural Language Processing projects requried the use and comparison of several neural networks. Molino defines his work as follows:

Ludwig is a toolbox built on top of TensorFlow that allows for training and testing of deep learning models without the need to write code.
-Getting Started (with Ludwig)

Install Ludwig within a Virtual Environment

Installing Ludwig is a trivial task, as long as a virtual environment is possible on the given system. Before following the Ludwig page installation instructions, it is important to ensure the virtual environment is set up for Python 3.6.9, as anything above this version will error out. Note here that a virtual environment already existed in this location, but python was set to the 3.6.9 version again.

Once the correct python version is set within the virtual environment, use the following line to install ludwig, per their installation guide found here . Optionally, you may also install visualization extensions for Ludwig by adding [viz] directly after ludwig in the command.

Create a model definition yaml file

Creating a model file for Ludwig is also exceedingly simple. Two sections are required, one for input and the other for output. Within each of these sections, unique variables are separated by the - character, with each variable having both a name and type. In this example, all input variables are considered as numerical, so each type of the input variables listed is 'numerical'. The output variables are similarly formatted, with name and type. Here, we are wanting to predict the presence (or absence) of a citation occuring, so the type is set to binary. This data is then saved as 'citation_model_definition.yaml' within our working directory. 

input_features
    	-
      		name: Year
      		type: category
    	-
      		name: Month
      		type: category
    	-
      		name: WeekDay
      		type: category
    	- 
      		name: Hour
      		type: category
    	-
      		name: Segment
      		type: category
    	-
      		name: Road_Num
      		type: category

output_features:
    	-
      		name: Citation
      		type: binary

Train a citation prediction model

With the newly created model file, we can now proceed to train a prediction model. Running the following command automatically trains the model on the data from the TotalData.csv file, utilizing the citation_model_definition.yaml file we just created. Additionally, the command begins the output of training statistics, and saves the results within the specified folder. Note: If you wish to save the output displayed in the terminal, add '>> Output.txt' to the end of the command. 

    ludwig train --data_format ‘csv’ 
    --training_set traindata.csv 
    --validation_set testdata.csv 
    --config citation_model_definition.yaml
The above command produces the following output in the terminal:

This is the automatic choice for the Ludwig toolbox, but everything is helpfully labeled once saved within the results folder. As the statistics reveal, the model began at moderate statistics, but then jumped to over 99% accuracy. This doesn't allow for too much improvement beyond that, but Ludwig has logic embedded within it that ends training when the accuracy does not improve over a given number of epochs, and we do see that Ludwig halted training after 11 epochs. This not only saves time, but also allows for an easier understood statistic file since it is reported when the accuracy fails to improve and the last time accuracy did improve.

Test a citation prediction model

After we've trained the model and taken a look at the statistics from it, we can test the model as well. This is completed by typing the following command into the terminal, as done before: 

    ludwig evaluate --test_set testdata.csv 
    --model-path results/experiment_run_0/model

At the end of the snippet above, we can see performance statistics of testing, with strong predictive power for negatives. Our model does struggle with predicting positives but that is common in these rare event prediction situations. Now, let's take a deeper look into our performance statistics.

Review Accuracy of Results

As with the training process, Ludwig saved some great statistics after the testing process. What is amazing is that the following snippet actually excludes some of the returned information for the sake of conciseness. The original json file (titled 'test_statistics.json') includes 977,834 instances each of Precision and Recall scores at the end of the included snippet.

All of the statistics one would ever be interested in are returned, with both "True" and "False" versions included. Now, let's take a look at just a few of the included performance markers from the file snippets we've seen. First, of course is training accuracy and loss. Note that as mentioned above, accuracy begins quite low and then jumps to over 99% on the second epoch. The same is said (of course in reverse) for loss. The loss begins rather high, then drops dramatically on the second epoch.

Finally, the ROC curve created by the testing statistics is shown, with quite a good ROC score being achieved. Note that this curve is created by over 296,000 individual values being supplied by the testing statistics file.

Discuss Issues and Drawbacks of Ludwig

It can't be overstated how helpful Ludwig could prove for model building enthusiasts. Being able to create a model without ever touching a code file is refreshing and pretty amazing. However, there are some drawbacks to the toolbox. As with many machine learning techniques, Ludwig is pretty much a black box to beginners. It can also be a bit tricky to get installed, as my MacBook Pro's inability to properly install the additional resources (mainly visualization) that are offered demonstrated. Of course, this could be down to user error, but following the tutorial did not prove to solve this issue. Also, while I was impressed with the 'automatic' creation of a model, training did take quite a bit longer than expected, and considerably more processing power in comparison to our last post's hand-built model. All in all, while Ludwig is most definitely a boon to professional model makers that know the ins and outs of their machines, it might be just a bit too hefty for a beginner to immediately pick up and run with.

Summary

In this example, we've walked through the installation and use of Ludwig, as well as reviewing its performance.
